1 EXCELLENCE
1.1 Main challenges and motivation 
Solar Heat for Industrial Processes (SHIP)  is becoming increasingly relevant as one of the ways to meet the high 
thermal energy demand required for industry. This involves a double benefit: first ly, by using a ren ewable energy 
source, fossil fuel consumption is reduced and therefore the emission of pollution and greenhouse gases  into the 
This proposal version was submitted by Antonio ROVIRA on 27/08/2019 13:57:53 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
  LC-RES-07-2019 
Page 2 
atmosphere; secondly, heat for industrial processes becomes a new market niche for solar technology , which can lead 
to a decrease in the cost of solar collectors through economies of scale  in manufacturing and learning-curve advances 
in deployment. 
 
According to a recent study by the National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL) of the U.S1., “the European Union 
(EU) has been a leader in the use, development, deployment, and tracking of SHIP plants in the last decade.” However, 
to date this has largely been limited to heat loads below 150 ºC. The next step in widescale adoption of process solar 
heating across the EU is to develop technologies and methodologies for reliable solar process heating above 150 ºC 
which will enlarge the exploitation possibilities. 
 
There are 111 operating SHIP systems in Europe, according to the SHIP-plant database of IEA Task 49 2, and only 17 
of them works at temperatures above 150 ºC, in only 6 EU countries. Additionally, there are no SHIP plants identified 
in Romania or Greece that are capable of supplying heat at temperatures above 150 º C. (KPI 1: up to 24h thermal 
energy production at 175 ºC (MAND) and 230 ºC (AMTP) . Thus, it is important to point out that the installations in 
this proposal are targeted at these countries to meet the requirement of the call of focussing in those EU countries where 
SHIP systems currently have very limited or no application. The selected industries cover SPIRE and non-SPIRE ones. 
 
It is important to note that not all the collectors are valid for the target temperature of the call: above 150 ºC. According 
to Fig. 1.1, this temperature can be obtained by means of parabolic trough or linear Fresnel collectors3. 
  
Fig. 1.1 Framework for ASTEP: typical industrial sectors for SHIP integration (SPIRE and NON SPIRE)
1.2 How ASTEP addresses the main challenges 
The comparably higher capital expenditure, lack of compact thermal storage and absence of suitable incentives has 
resulted in a considerable gap between the utilization potential and presently installed capacity of Solar Heat Industrial 
Process (SHIP)4 systems. The ASTEP project tackles these barriers by improving the feasibility of the solar thermal 
systems thanks to two features. The proposed system, namely SunDial is based on the Linear Fresnel Reflector (LFR) 
technology, which is considered as the most promising one for reducing the CAPEX. However, the system has a rotary 
structure instead of a linear one. Therefore, the first feature is the  modularity of the Sun Dial, which can make the 
system more easily installed in the industry, as well as contribut ing to the cost reduction. The second feature is the 
possibility of introducing a two-axis tracking system without a large increase in the cost, which allows a performance 
improvement that should lead to higher operating temperatures or to increase the feasibility in regions with high 
latitude, characteristic in most of the E U countries. KPI 2: n ew technological frontier for Romania : 220 ºC at hi gh 
latitude 
 
Thus, SunDial is designed to meet all the characteristics that are required for SHIP collectors5, and which are also 
demanded in the specific challenge of this call: 
 
1 https://www.nrel.gov/docs/fy16osti/64709.pdf 
2 http://ship-plants.info/ 
3 MJ Montes et al. SolarPaces 2018 
4 https://doi.org/10.1016/j.rser.2017.04.079

• Modularity: easy/fast collector installation , repair/replacement and disassembly procedures as well as the 
potential for hydraulic field layouts with low costs for connecting parts and low pressure drop. (KPIs 1,2) 
• Robustness and safety: reliable and safe operation under the conditions of an industrial environment. 
• Operation and maintenance requirements: the operation and maintenance procedures must be accessible to 
end-user technical personnel without special training in solar technologies . (KPI 3: Fixed mirrors limiting 
maintenance and operation requirements in MAND). 
• Integration into existing processes: compatibility with pre-existing (hydraulic) circuits, by the use of common 
and standard components or Heat Transfer Fluids (HTFs). (KPI 4: No changes in industrial processes due to 
the solar thermal contribution). 
 
As it was commented above, the solar concept presented can even provide process heat to industries located at different 
latitudes, which is an additional advantage over conventional LFR systems. For that reason, two End Users located in 
two European countries at different latitudes will be studied: Romania and Greece. ASTEP also seek for minimum 
changes in actual industr ial processes (KPI 4), so a compact (to preserve modularity as much as possible) thermal 
storage system is integrated, based on phase change materials (PCM). 
 
A latent thermal energy storage system will be installed, in order to accommodate the delay between solar energy 
supply and heat demand. Different phase change materials (PCM) will be considered for the temperature levels of the 
End Users requirements. The development of a storage system based on hybrid active-passive heat transfer techniques 
will be developed in the frame of ASTEP project.  This enables its manageability in relatively small sizes, as required 
by industrial sites.  
These systems must be integrated in a global control system, which includes not only the collector and the storage, but 
also heat exchanger and absorption chillers in the case of refrigeration requirements.  
 
Finally, another characteristic of the solar collector that is proposed is that it can be adapted to different industrial 
processes demanding heat at different temperatures. The process temperatures for different industrial sectors can be: 
low (T < 100  ºC), medium (100 ºC < T < 2 50 ºC) or high temperature (250  ºC < T < 400  ºC). The typical industrial 
processes where solar heat is in use are: water heating; drying processes; preheating processes; steam heating process; 
pasteurization and sterilization process; washing processes6 7. 
 
For the temperature required in the call (above 150 ºC), only medium-high temperature processes are valid. In this 
project, two applications have been selected: 
• Pasteurization process in a food industry in Greece (37.93 N) with a temperature demand of 170 ºC. (the provision 
of solar-driven chilling, typically requiring a heat source of at ~140 ºC) will also be included at this site). 
• Preheating process in a fabricated metal industry in Romania (latitude 47.1 N), with a temperature demand above 
220 ºC. 
 
The proposed solar concept will be adapted to the different processes  of the two industries , at different temperatures 
and located at different latitudes. This is one of the main advantages of ASTEP: its versatility to provide heat at very 
different conditions and thermal loads even within the same industry, thanks to reliable solar heat production and a 
suitable management of the energy thanks to the thermal storage system.  
 
In addition, the modularity of the proposed concentrator may lead to a fast application of the concepts to different  
industries and different sizes. Multiple concentrator modules can be installed in parallel with a common storage within 
an integrated ASTEP system. This will aid to the standardization of the system, which will be done during the project 
in order to allow ease of replication.

respective WPs and performance indicators, later used as milestones (MS). The objectives are the following ones , 
shown in Table 1.2: 
 
The main objective of ASTEP project is to successfully demonstrate the viability of a pplying solar 
thermal energy to partially cover heating and heating and cooling demands on two different relevant 
industrial demo sites located on two different climate regions  (Iasi in Romania an d Corinth in Greece)  
and to further develop the implementation of solar thermal energy in industrial processes up to 400ºC.

1.4 Concept and methodology 
1.4.1 1.4.1 Concept 
ASTEP project includes several innovative technologies that address the scope of the call, namely a low -cost 
concentrator able to achieve temperatures well above 150 ºC and a hybrid storage system specifically designed for the 
requirements of the case studies. These technologies are integrated in the ASTEP system in order to give flexibility to 
the end users in a centrally controlled operation. 
 
SunDial: A new concentrator concept for solar heat for industrial processes.  
Solar concentrators for industrial processes should be simpler than those designed for power generation, as heat transfer 
fluid rates are normally lower, and the maintenance of the concentrator cannot require too much cost. Solar 
concentrators for solar heat for industrial processes generally include non -imaging concentrators (for relatively low 
temperatures), linear Fresnel collectors and parabolic trough. The two latter are able to achieve temperatures well above 
150 ºC, but they are systems adapted from power generation with high maintenance cost. In this project, an innovative 
solar technology concept is proposed: the S unDial. This concept has the main objective of limiting installation, 
maintenance and operation requirements, as demanded in the specific challenge of this call. It is the result of four 
Spanish patents  ES2578804B2, ES1138715U, ES2537607B2 and ES2713799A1, and two international patents 
WO/2016/166388A1 and WO/2016/166390A1, which belong to UPM and UNED. 
 
The SunDial is a Rotary Fresnel Collector (RFC) that consists of a horizontal platform that rotates a round a vertical 
axis, with a linear concentrator installed on the platform. The solar field consists of mirrors of circular cross section 
This proposal version was submitted by Antonio ROVIRA on 27/08/2019 13:57:53 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
  LC-RES-07-2019 
Page 7 
parallel to the receiver line which are used as concentrator. As explained later, these mirrors are obtained from fla t 
mirror which are bent on site, simplifying the installation. Two designs of the SunDial device are considered. 
 
In the first one, the working condition for this Fresnel system is that the sun must be in the symmetry plane of the 
concentrator, i.e. the system must rotate in the azimuth angle along the day. In th is design, the mirrors are fixed to the 
structure (KPI 3). This feature, together with the small curvature of the mirrors, allows the installation of flat mirrors 
slightly curved during the mounting process by a simple clamp. The compactness of the system and the fixed mirrors 
will lead to a low-cost solution with a high focussing accuracy. 
 
In the second one, Sundial consists of several rows of rotating mirrors (instead of fixed ones). Mirrors and receiver are 
installed onto the rotating platform that turn around a vertical axis to maintain the sun within the collector transversal 
plane. At the same time, mirrors will rotate around their longitudinal axis in order to reflect onto the receiver as the sun 
altitude varies. Thus, the system follows the Sun using a two -axis tracking system, i.e. the platform rotates with the 
sun’s azimuthal angle and the tilting angle of the mirrors change following the solar altitude. This solutio n shares the 
advantages of the previous one regarding the cost reduction, simplicity and compactness. The additional tracking 
system (tilt of the mirrors) introduces some complexity –and the corresponding cost - but must lead to very high 
performance that allows the use of Fresnel technology at altitudes that conventional technologies cannot operate  (KPI 
2). 
 
The accuracy required for the sun tracking when rotating around the vertical axis is obtained with commercial wheels 
for scaffoldings and other industrial applications. A suitably resistant round track is needed to that end. In order to 
avoid the horizontal displacement of the rotary Fresnel, two alternatives are possible: 
• The use of a central axial bearing, which stands part of the platform weight and avoids its displacement.  
• The use of a set of wheels with vertical axis around the platform, as depicted in Fig. 1.2 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 1.2 Conceptual basic design of a small-scale RFC 
 
As it was commented above, t he cost reduction potential of this concept is increased by a new methodology to bend 
mirrors, which has been introduced by Spanish patent ES2596294B2, from U PM and UNED. This methodology for 
the use of thin fla t mirrors, which are very cheap compared to the mirrors used in parabolic trough collectors 
(manufactured with the permanent curvature required for the application). On the contrary, following this methodology 
standard flat mirrors are bent with a special but simple device that can be adjusted to obtain the curved mirrors with a 
circular cross-section and a specific radius of curvature. 
 
A way to apply a bending moment at each beam end is depicted in Fig. 1.3. Two rods at each side, one above and 
another below the glass of the mirror, are rotated until reaching the slope predicted by the former analysis. Both rods 
are pushed by a radial arm turning around a pivot. Once the mirror has got its shape, the pusher is kept fixed. 
  
Fig. 1.3: Cross-section of a bent mirror by torques applied to both lateral sides (left) and upper view of the bent mirror 
(right) 
 
Fig. 1.4 shows a different embodiment for applying the same bending moments. In this case the rotating levers are not 
placed at the tips of a mirror, but on the sides. The rotating pivot is in the rear face of the mirror, and the lever acts on 
the upper rod. 
This proposal version was submitted by Antonio ROVIRA on 27/08/2019 13:57:53 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
  LC-RES-07-2019 
Page 8 
 
 
Fig. 1.4: Cross-section of a bent mirror by torques applied to both lateral sides with lateral levers (left) and upper view of 
the bent mirror (right) 
 
This new concept for bending mirrors has been proved at laboratory scale. A 3 m long 1 m wide strip has been 
manufactured from 3 mm flat mirrors.  A pair of levers has been installed every 50 cm and the bending moment has 
been adjusted by means of a pair of threaded rods, one of them counter threaded. 
 
A simple test has been carried out at UPM in order to proof t his 
concept. The sun has been concentrated onto a vertical wall at a 
distance of around 8 m, similar to the distance found in a linear 
Fresnel collector. In order to prove that the concept is adequate for 
linear concentrators, the last rotating levers wher e not adjusted. The 
shape of the reflected sunlight can be observed in Fig. 1.5. 
 
One can observe that the first 2 m of mirrors concentrate the sunlight 
onto a narrow line. However, this line is slightly blurred in the 50 cm 
between two consecutive rotating levers. In order to reduce this effect 
a higher number of rotating lever pairs should be used. One can also 
observe that the last meter is not concentrated onto a narrow line. 
This is due to the fact that the last rotating levers had not been 
adjusted. T herefore, it can be concluded that this bending 
methodology is adequate for the SunDial system and, thus, ordinary 
thin flat mirrors can be used.  
 
Fig. 1.5. Sun concentration on a 
vertical wall from bending mirrors, 
proof of concept by UPM 
 
Finally, SunDial concept must include a way to remove heat from  the rotating platform in order to be used by the 
industrial process. This can be done by means of a rotating joint, as described in patent ES 2504916B2, or even by a 
rotating heat exchanger, as described in patent ES2437267A1.  
Overall, it can be said the current TRL level is TRL3: first laboratory tests have been completed, see Fig. 5, although 
measurements for a laboratory prototype scale have not been obtained yet. This project expects to bring the technology 
to a TRL 5, including the construction of a lab prototype and installation and application at the end-user.  
 
Thermal Storage 
A hybrid thermal energy storage system, consisting of the combination of passive and active heat transfer enhancement 
techniques, is to be developed with the aim of accumulating the excess of solar thermal energy during peak production 
hours, and to deliver it through the end user’s demand under controlled power conditions. 
 
The passive design comprises the application of the honeycomb structures which are located in a shell enclosure and 
have the form of multi tubes with integrated elements enhancing the heat transfer. The heat transfer fluid (HTF) flows 
through the inside tubes, and the PCM is stored at the shell side, filling the structure created by the honeycomb. 
 
The innovative active design makes possible the control of the stored thermal energy durin g charging and released 
during discharging. An uneven design of the honeycomb structure allows to establish sectors with different relations 
between heat transfer area and PCM locally stored, namely surface -to-volume ratios. The possibility of the flow 
distribution system to control the mass flow of HTF, by means of a sectorized regulation of the hydraulic resistance, 
allows for adjusting the thermal power of the LTES system during both charging and discharging cycles. 
 
It is planned to design and execute two energy storage units: one will be based on PCM characterized by a lower 
melting point, above 150 °C, and for this unit, as is shown in Table 1.3, rather multi-component mixture of salts is 
preferred as a PCM material. Selection of  molar ratio of mixtures allows to tailor its melting range, which at eutectic 
composition reaches minimal value. The most promising material, with high latent heat, is salt-salt mixture of NaNO3-
LiNO3. The second PCM is foreseen for higher working temperatures above 230 °C and can be based on single or multi 
component PCM. According to the fact that both PCM materials are characterized by a relatively low thermal 
conductivity, the metal inserts from the  Al-Si alloys will be applied, in order to increase the heat transfer. Mutual 
This proposal version was submitted by Antonio ROVIRA on 27/08/2019 13:57:53 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
  LC-RES-07-2019 
Page 9 
interaction, usually corrosion or dissolution, between metal and salts must be limited. Preliminary examinations 
confirmed no reaction of NaNO3 and KNO3 with aluminium alloy or steel. 
Table 1.3. Thermophysical properties of inorganic Phase Change Materials [891011] 
Material Melting temp [°C] Latent heat [kJ/kg] Thermal conductivity [W/mK] 
NaNO3 295-315 172-182 0.51 
KNO3 330-333 266 0.42 
NaOH 293-318 159-165 0.66

Another key factor limiting the application of some forms of metallic inserts is the issue of their fatigue failure during 
the cyclic phase transitions of the PCM materials. When the metallic inserts are subjected to both changing stresses 
and elastic stra ins, the metal parts (metallic foams ) deform severely after a high number of loading cycles. This 
drawback will be eliminated by the application of specific design of metal inserts, based on the honeycomb structure, 
ensuring high stiffness, good fatigue resistance and significantly prolonged lifetime. 
 
Integrated ASTEP System Concept 
Solar thermal is fundamental for meeting forthcoming thermal needs while decarbonising the industrial sector. Its 
deployment has experienced a powerful expansion during the past years mainly due to a strong cost reduction and 
supportive policies. Neverthel ess, the intrinsic variability of solar energy raises new challenges for operators and 
regulators of thermal systems and the feasibility of this technology is also strongly influenced on the economics of 
energy/fuels markets. 
One of the decisive aspects fo r an optimal integration of a SHIP system is its flexibility, which enables it to maintain 
continuous service against rapid and large swings in supply or demand. In order to balance the implicit intermittent 
nature of solar energy and guarantee that heat supply remains within process specifications  (temperature, pressure, 
flow rates) a bespoke integration of modular components will be accomplished for each application, allowing for an 
optimized energy delivery to the industrial process. 
The integration requ ires technical solutions 
such as centralised control and flexible 
operation of the system; improvements to solar 
energy generation predictions; enhancement 
of operational tools and practices; and 
development of technologies supporting 
demand response.  (See Fig. 1.6) 
 
Thermal energy storage allows the mitigation 
of the effects of intermittent renewable 
resources on the networks and actual hydraulic 
circuits.  
 
Fig. 1.6: Schematic of the Integrated ASTEP System Concept 
 
ASTEP  will validate the integration of these concepts in two industrial case studies demonstrating the viability of the 
proposed solutions for different industries located at different latitudes, which will enable the use of the developed 
technology in EU countries where concentrating solar systems h ave very limited or no application. Validation will 
be carried out installing a single module of 17 kWth (peak) in each industry, able to produce from 50 kWh per day in 
winter to 135 kWh per day in summer (KPI 5). This performance should lead to a solar contribution of 25 MWh yearly,

avoiding 5.7 t of CO2 to the atmosphere (KPI 6), saving 2 t of NG (KPI 7) and 5 kg of NOx (compared to uncontrolled 
burners) (KPI 8), in each industry . The module size (8 m diameter) is  selected to use the most common standard 
components in the solar industry (evacuated and coated tubes) and thin mirrors with a width and length small enough 
to be manufactured in conventional furnaces in glass industry. 
• Case Study 1: MANDREKAS (MAND). 
The company MANDREKAS is a family owned dairy located in Corinth (latitude is 37.93 N), i.e. it is located 
close to the sun Belt, where solar concentrating systems can be installed at a lower price. They produce all types 
of yogurt, yogurt-based dressings (tzatziki sauce) and milk desserts (rice pudding, custard cream and chocolate). 
In their process they need both the generation of steam at 8 bar (175 ºC), to pasteurize milk, and cooling for storing 
their products at temperatures around 5 ºC.  
The concentrato r developed for this case study must be designed so that capital and maint enance costs are 
minimized. In order to do so, a symmetric concentrator is proposed, so that the vertical rotation axis belongs to the 
symmetry plane. The working condition for this Fresnel system is that the sun must be in the symmetry plane of 
the concentrator, i.e. the system must rotate in the azimuth angle along the day. Therefore, the platform must be 
placed over some sets of wheels that are guided in a circular movement. 
Mirrors are fixed in a planar platform that rotates tracking the sun in the azimuth angle. T he tilt of every mirror 
that is defined in the reference position (when the receiver and the Sun define the virtual symmetry plane of the 
system). By minimizing the moving parts, the maintenance and operation is also very simple, achieving a highly 
reliable system. 
This system always leads to a null incident transversal angle which implies absence of shading or blocking losses 
and, more importantly, it implies that mirrors do not require an individual tracking system each. The mirrors are 
continuously in the same relative position from the receiver, placed in the general focal line, which is in the middle 
of the narrow bundle of focal lines of each individual mirror. As a result, the system becomes very stiff and simple. 
In fact, it is always the same system , where the only object changing along the day is the Sun. The focusing 
geometry is therefore invariant, and the system can be featured as a function of the zenithal angle of the Sun 
measured in the alignment plane, i.e. the symmetry plane. 
Although the incident transversal angle is zero, the incidence longitudinal angle becomes very important when the 
sun altitude is low, i.e. during the morning and afternoon. In order to reduce the cosine factor effect, which reduces 
the energy impinging onto the concentrator, tilting the Fresnel field installed on the RFC towards the Sun becomes 
highly advisable. It must be noted, nevertheless, that the platform size does not have strict limitations, but the tilting 
angle: if the platform diameter is too large, the height  in the furthest can make the system too expensive. A trade -
off between radiation capture increase and system complexity must be worked out to define the optimal tilting. 
The incident longitudinal angle affects not only to the cosine factor, but also to end losses. This is why the concept 
includes a set of transversal mirrors, as specified in Spanish patent ES2449167B2. A transversal mirror system can 
be fixed in a position perpendicular to the primary mirrors, reducing end losses. However, in order to avoi d high 
wind loads they can be assembled as vertical strips which rotate around a vertical axis, so that they are left parallel 
to the wind direction when its speed overtakes a given threshold.  
As previously said, this very low -cost concentrator implies and important variation of the cosine factor along the 
day. Therefore, the storage system becomes especially relevant to meet heat demand requirements. On the other 
hand, these requirements are relatively modest because only the cooling demand is 24/7, where as the steam 
generation must be met only during the day. With the industrial process temperature being 175  ºC, most efforts 
will be done to develop the storage system based on salt-salt mixture of NaNO3-LiNO3, which has been identified 
as the most promising mixture in this temperature range.  
In order to meet cooling requirements, a commercial absorption chiller will be used. This system must be able to 
work with a heat supply at the same conditions than the industrial process (175 ºC), so that a completely integrated 
system is developed. KPIs 5 to 8. 
• Case Study 2: ArcelorMittal. (AMTP) 
ArcelorMittal is the world’s leading steel and mining company . In particular, AM Iasi, which belongs to Europe 
Division ArcelorMittal Tubular Products (AMTP), is engaged with the manufacturing of welded steel tub es for 
many diverse applications . This industry is located at Iasi, latitude 47.1 N. O ne of the key finishing for the AM 
Iasi’s products is the so -called colour coating, consisting on a thin layer of (epoxy, water -based, thermoplastic, 
etc.) coloured protective/decorative material covering the whole tube external surface. In order to apply this colour 
coating tubes, they need to be pre-heated to a temperature of 220 ºC.  
When the solar concentrator is to be installed in relatively high latitude locations, as it is the case of this case study, 
a slightly more complex system must be used. In this case, the working condition for this Fresnel system is that the 
sun must be in the transversal plane of the concentrator, i.e. the system must rotate in the azimuth a ngle along the 
This proposal version was submitted by Antonio ROVIRA on 27/08/2019 13:57:53 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
  LC-RES-07-2019 
Page 11 
day so that the longitudinal component of the im pinging sun radiation is null. Therefore, the platform must be 
placed over some sets of wheels that are guided in a circular movement. 
Due to the fact that the sun altitude varies along the day  and the year, mirrors must be installed on to longitudinal 
rotating axis, so that their transversal tilt is adapted to the sun altitude. This implies a higher cost compared to the 
one-axe tracking system, but it eliminates both cosine and end losses. As a result, the impinging thermal power 
variation will only be due to shading losses, which are less important than cosine and end losses when the sun 
altitude is very low. In addition, it eliminates the need for a platform fixed longitudinal tilt and transversal mirrors, 
which imply a cost reduction. 
Storage system requirements are less demanding with the two-axis concentrator, as the impinging power variation 
is lower thanks to the nearly constant cosine factor. However, industrial process temperatures are hi gher, which 
might lead to a more complex storage system where corrosion or dilution must be regarded. KPIs 5 to 8 
 
Positioning of the project 
The main activities of the ASTEP project will bring the technology from the first laboratory test completed ( TRL 3) 
to a large-scale prototype tested in intended environments (TRL 5). 
The basis of the technology has been developed and tested at laboratory scale separately for some of the  partners and 
considering other applications like power generation or building heating. One of the main challenges of the projects is 
the integration and adaption of these technologies for supplying heating and cooling to the process industry.

1.4.2 1.3.2 Methodology 
The methodology involves cooperation and fluent communication among the different partners working in their 
specialisation field. It goes from industrial partners needs to technological developers and comes back to the industrial 
partners with the integrated system developed for the involved end users. 
 
The methodology starts from the collected innovation potentials and the initial research of the different partners to 
develop a new concept to cover the heating and/or cooling demand of the end -users, which will be disseminated, 
exploited and communicated (Fig. 7). 
 
Fig. 7: ASTEP methodology 
 
The development of the ASTEP integrated concept will follow the next steps: 
1. Development of thermal demand profiles for the two end-users. 
2. Dimensioning of the SunDial for both processes, involving the following tasks: 
o Optical characterisation: Montecarlo Raytracing method. 
o Thermal characterisation: Aided by In-house codes and CFD tools. 
o Mechanical and structural assessment, using analytical and Finite Element Method (FEM) approaches. 
3. Dimensioning of the TES for both processes, defining the optimal honeycomb arrangement, aided by CFD tools. 
4. A dynamic model of the ASTEP system will be developed in the project by integrating the different component 
sub-models under a simulation environment. Furthermore, optimization algorithms and Thermal Demand Side 
Response (TDSR) principles will be established to achieve the most optimal configuration of energy generation 
and storage based on user case profiles. Also, a statistical assessment will be used to design the integrated system 
with targets for either Thermal Loss of Load Probability (TLOLP, %), or Solar Thermal Penetration (STP, %) in 
the case of a system with auxiliary heat. 
5. Construction and test of the solar collectors and TES in order to analyse these systems at a Lab Scale (WP6). 
6. Models revision from the test data and detailed design of the final systems to be installed at industrial sites.  
7. Transport and local construction of the auxiliary system and elements dedicated to integrating the system into the 
process. This process will be supervised by technological partners, as the quality control an accuracy for this step 
is critical for the success of the project. 
8. The whole process will be repeated over (at least) 2 iterative sequential testing cycles  to ensure that potential 
improvements will be taken into consideration in the re-design of the system after the first testing cycle. 
 
Gender analysis 
Nowadays, there still is an important gender gap in Science, Technology, Engineering and Mathematics (STEM), which 
is a social global problem. Gender equality is a value to warrant democracy, quality and development.  In the case of 
ASTEP project, all the partners are committed to promote gender balance at a ll levels, and to follow the H2020 
recommendations for gender equality.

Particularly important, it is the unbalanced representation of women in all the decision-making bodies, which is typical 
for SET (Science, Engineering, Technology) fields all around the world.  
 
ASTEP project is aligned with this idea and has included three women within its Executive Board, with important 
roles: Monica Osorio (EXE) as Exploitation Manager, Ruth Herrero-Martín (UPCT) as IPR and Dissemination 
Manager, and Kimberly Cornfield (CHX) as Communication Manager . This is a sign of the commitment of the

Another weakness of the gender equality is the cultural climate and stereotypes tending to underrate the importance of 
gender issues. 
 
ASTEP project partners will actively promote equal opportunities between men and women and, as far as possible , 
gender balance will be sought at all levels of personnel assignment. Also, ASTEP activities will be guided by the 
conclusions and recommendations of some EU CORDIS projects launched with the objective of analysing this gender 
gap, such as the project Gender Equality in Engineering through Communication and Commitment (GEECCO). During 
ASTEP execution, we will follow the Directive 2002/73/EC on the imp lementation of equal treatment for men and 
women as guideline, e.g. for access to employment, vocational training and promotion and working conditions.
1.5 Ambition 
ASTEP has several innovative components which will contribute to the progress of the current state of the art and turn 
the Europe into a leading player in the renewable energy market, in line with the objectives of the EC Communication 
"Renewable Energy: a major player in the European energy market", the SET-Plan and the expected objectives of the 
announced Commission's 2013 Communication on energy technology policy. 
 
1.5.1 Carrying the technology beyond the state-of-art 
ASTEP introduces four innovation lines. They are presented below starting by the description of the current state -of-
the-art and then introducing the contributions introduced by ASTEP. Namely they are: (1) SunDial; (2) Thermal 
Storage System; (3) Integrated ASTEP concept; and (4) Case Studies. 
• INNOVATION LINE 1: SunDial 
The use of solar thermal energy in industry and tertiary sector is currently insignificant, even though this application 
has a huge technical and commercial potential, about 30% of the overall energy demand in the partner countr ies 
originates in the industrial sector, mainly as heat at temperature levels below  250 °C. In several specific industry 
sectors, such as food and beverages, transport equipment, machinery, textiles, the share of heat demand at low and 
medium temperatures (below 250 °C) is around 60%. Tapping into this potential would provide a significant solar 
contribution to industrial energy requirements. 
The main focus in this project will be on activities which support the decrease of cost, increases the modularity of 
systems and which supports the increase of the thermal sup ply. Also, the improvement of electronic controllers 
will be a focal point. 
A key guideline towards commercial competitiveness of SHIP is to reduce the mechanical and thermal complexity 
of the solar field configuration and to reduce the specific or unitary costs of the elements required for absorb ing 
the concentrated solar flux as thermal energy of a working fluid in the receiver. An imp ortant hint in this quest is 
not to oversize the system. This is particularly applicable to the concentration factor achieved in the receiver, which 
must not exceed too much the threshold value for getting a given final temperature in the thermal fluid.  
The Rotary Fresnel Collector meets the above requirements. This new configuration is based on circular-cylindrical 
mirrors, which can easily be made from planar ordinary mirror by applying a bending technique that is also very 
simple and very cheap. The ful l system is fixed on a planar platform that rotates around a vertical axis as the sun 
moves, in such a way that the receiver line, which is in the vertical plane of symmetry of the mirror field, keeps 
always a perfect alignment with the sun. 
Fresnel collectors had a late development compared to the other technologies: even though a few papers and patents 
on Fresnel lens, enhanced flat collectors and compound systems were published in the mid-1970s, there is no well-
known published work until the second half  of the 1980s, when oil prices reached minimum values. After energy 
crises, interest in CSP technologies was diluted, and therefore, neither Fresnel prototypes nor commercial plants 
were built during this period, researchers being less active in this domain. 
Nowadays, Fresnel technology seems to have a great potential to reduce the Levelized Cost of Energy (LCOE) in 
CSP. This is due to the fact that lineal Fresnel collectors have obvious economic advantages when compared to 
other technologies: (1) Only one receiver is used for the whole array of mirrors, and it is fixed. As a result, no 
rotating joints or flexible hoses are required, and therefore no environment or health issues due to hot oil leakages 
must be prevented. In addition, thanks to this configura tion the tracked structure is lighter and thus the rotating 
mechanism is simpler. (2) There is no need for glass -metal welds, which often cause receiver ruptures due to 
This proposal version was submitted by Antonio ROVIRA on 27/08/2019 13:57:53 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
  LC-RES-07-2019 
Page 14 
dilatation coefficient differences. Therefore, the receiver is more robust. (3) Mirrors  are less expensive and easier 
to clean thanks to their low curvature.  Although most of the comparative studies between Parabolic Troughs and 
Fresnel are focused in electricity production (not relevant in ASTEP), there is another application for which Fresnel 
collectors are particularly attractive, which is to generate thermal energy for industrial processes, in the temperature 
range 150 ºC-400 ºC. 
For industrial applications, the Rotary Fresnel Collector is even more attractive, as the investment cost is r educed 
regards to a conventional Fresnel, and the operation and maintenance is simple and reliable. 
• INNOVATION LINE 2: Thermal storage 
On the other hand, most current scientific and technological reports describing future scenarios of the European 
energy landscape agree that energy storage will be the one of the main tools to support the energy transition. The 
application of phase change materials (PCM) for solar thermal energy storage is a promising technology for solving 
the time delay between thermal energy production and consumption. Importantly, latent PCM has multiple times 
storage capacity compared to sensible heat materials, rangin g from 50 to 150 kWh/t with efficiency between 75 
and 90%.  
Despite these advantages, the integration of latent thermal energy storage (LTES) systems in solar thermal facilities 
still lacks some technological features that prevent their technical and econo mic feasibility: typical heat transfer 
configurations for high temperature latent thermal energy storage comprise the use of shell and tube arrangements, 
owing to its robust construction geometry. Conversely, the limited surface-to-volume ratio yields very low charging 
and discharging velocities, which results in low heat transfer effectiveness of this type of LTES design. 
Additionally, this configuration does not allow the separation of capacity and power. 
As pointed out by EASE EERA, the development of active heat exchange systems with a controllable heat transfer 
power is of paramount importance for the broad implementation in the market of the LTES technology. 
The use of honeycomb inserts, as energy storage concept for this proposal, is intended for incr easing the surface-
to-volume ratio in the storage system. At the same time, the mechanical integrity of the honeycomb, compared to 
other inserts like metallic foams, will ensure the null impact of the thermal cycling on the long -term effectiveness 
of the system. 
The hybrid nature of the thermal storage system to be implemented accounts for an active control of the power 
release, based on the regulation of the mass flow distribution across the multi-tube arrangement. This is of special 
interest for designing a LTES system with separated thermal capacity and power. 
Application of metallic 3D Al -Si based structures is one of the most promising solutions for the enhancement of 
the heat transfer from the solar cells into the PCM materials in the proposed heat acc umulators. It is important to 
emphasize that sophisticated solutions or new materials for improving the heat transfer are limited in their industrial 
application due to economic issues. Therefore, any optimization procedure that could lead to desirable res ults in 
terms of the multidirectional heat transfer would be highly appreciated for their reliable performance. Manufactured 
structures immersed in a working PCM medium are suitable to improve the heat transfer, efficiency and the overall 
performance of the heat accumulators. They can be applied in a form of compact units conducting the heat energy 
due to their high heat transfer surface area per unit volume and the relatively large value of heat conduction. 
Modeled geometry and dimensions of metal inserts in terms of thermal performance will be produced and 
experimentally validated. 
• INNOVATION LINE 3: Integrated ASTEP System Concept 
The proposal adopts an integrated approach  that involves modular and flexible design integration process 
involving Sundial, thermal storage, heat pump and overall control strategy for a final ASTEP model. Conventional 
technologies currently used in Industries for the Thermal demand use fossil fuel as the primary source of energy, 
despite their high energy conversion factors they emit significant amount of CO2 and pollutants to the environment. 
The major advantage of ASTEP system is its primary source of energy is solar power, which is sustainable and 
renewable. Existing solar energy-based technologies used in industries for thermal demand (heating and or cooling) 
perform in a varying proportion depending on the daytime and season, thus impose high requirements of  solar 
energy as a main source. 
On the other hand, solar thermal technologies are oriented towards the production of heat and or cold at small and 
intermediate scales and are usually complemented by using a backup boiler, which makes the system more costly, 
complex and difficult to maintain. 
The new ASTEP integration process address the foreseen problem and progress beyond state-of-the-art Innovation 
potential.  
Specific tailored designs and control strategies addressing to individual user demand profiles, location data, max 
and min thermal demand, operational times. 
Modular and flexible integration model fo r different user case scenarios (Level 1 & 2) covering various industry 
applications – ease of upscaling to higher capacity. 
This proposal version was submitted by Antonio ROVIRA on 27/08/2019 13:57:53 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
  LC-RES-07-2019 
Page 15 
Compactness in design, all the equipment’s can be located outdoors, on the building roof or on a free lot, thus 
sparring valuable plant or industry floor. 
The flexibility and integration level of the technology allows its amortization in lower periods than conventional 
solar heating/cooling technologies. 
• INNOVATION LINE 4: Case Studies 
All the previous Innovation streams are applied to the fourth Line, the Case Studies: 
The main and direct innovation in the Case Studies, i.e., the two selected industries, relies on the integration of 
solar energy into the industrial process. In both cases, Greek dairy industry (MAND) and Romanian steel industry 
(AMTP), the current energy demand is covered with non -renewable energy sources. ASTEP will introduce into 
them reliable solar heat. 
In the case of MAND, the conventional solar system to be integrated would be a parabolic trough collectors’ solar 
field or a conventional LFR systems. 
Compared to them, ASTEM is low demanding in terms of space requirements (no necessarily linear) and a more 
robust system with fixed mirrors avoiding lots of moving parts. Thus, as an additional innovation,  the expected 
advance is a more reliable operation and reduction of cost. 
In the case of AMTP, placed at high latitude, currently there are not systems able to supply reliable solar thermal 
power at temperatures around 230 ºC at a reasonable cost. 
Thanks to its compact rotary platform, ASTEP allows the introduction of a second tracking system, changing the 
tilt of the mirrors, to improve the performance of the system. The expected advance is to achieve temperatures that 
other systems cannot reach at the specific latitude and reasonable cost. 
Furthermore, thanks to the thermal storage system and a suitable energy management, ASTEP avoids any 
adaptation of the actual industrial processes.

resulted in a considerable breach between the utilization potential and presently installed capacity of  SHIP systems.

The ASTEP project will tackle the barriers by improving the feasibility of the solar thermal systems thanks to an 
improved collector design, the implementation of a thermal storage system and the adapted integration of all the 
systems. 
 
Solar collector innovation . As commented before, the SunDial is a novel concept that introduces important 
innovations over the current state -of-the-art of Linear Fresnel Reflector technology  (KPIs 2 -4). As it has a rotary 
structure, its design is modular, feature that provide flexibility and easy adaptation to the land or the space available in 
the specific industry. The use of flat mirrors curved during the mounting process contributes to the cost reduction. 
Finally, the possibility of incorporat ing a second axis tracking system without a large increase of the cost allows a 
performance improvement that should lead to either higher operating temperatures or larger operation or, most 
important, to increase the feasibility in regions with high latitude, characteristic in most of the EU countries (KPI 2). 
All the commented features are protected and published in the patents ES2578804B2, ES1138715U (WO2016166390), 
ES2537607B2 (WO2016166388) and ES2713799A1. In these documents, the corresponding patent search was done 
to demonstrate the novelty of the proposed systems. For example, WO2005124245, WO2002097341, WO2007109901 
and WO2009121174 present rotary systems to concentrate solar energy, although they are different from the Sundial. 
Besides, the patents  ES2370567, ES2232232A1, ES2299109T3 and ES2419529 present conventional procedures to 
curve mirrors by means of fixing the glass to a structure, but the solution used in SunDial is different. 
 
Thermal Storage innovation. The hybrid thermal storage systems implemented will highly contribute to the target of 
maximizing the share of covering the maximum heating and or cooling demand  (KPI 1). Regarding the energy storage 
market, it will see rapid expansion in the next years and decades: the global market is forecast to grow to at least $250 
billion by 2040. With this massive growth comes a unique opportunity for the European energy storage industry to 
ramp up the production of technologies and provision of associated services in Europe and abroad. In do ing so, the 
energy storage industry could contribute to re -industrialising Europe, contributing to long -term growth for European 
citizens while supporting the EU’s ambition to make Europe the world number one in renewables. 
 
Integration innovation. ASTEP project will implement specific tailored designs and control strategies addressing to 
individual user demand profiles, location data, max and min thermal demand, operational times.  The introduction of 
thermal storage for SHIP plants and its operation at hig h latitudes with a thermal demand at temperatures above 150 
ºC is a target for the introduction of innovative solutions in integration.  
 
Simulation innovation. In addition, ASTEP will use Finite Element Analysis (FEA) and Computational Fluid Dynamics 
(CFD) to provide structural, thermal and fluid analysis to optimise the solar collector and the STE system which is also 
an innovation into SHIP systems.

expected points planned on LC-SC3-RES-7 but also for the competitiveness and for the society.

An increased decarbonisation of the industrial sector. Due to the long investment cycles in industry, the next phase 
of decarbonisation will  include  thinking  about  how  industry  can  further  contribute  to  reducing  CO 2 emissions. 
According to the IEA, direct industrial CO2 emissions rose 0.3% to reach 8.5 GtCO2 in 2017 (24% of global emissions). 
ASTEP is aligned with the Sustainable Development Scenario (SDS), contributing to achieve the emission 
decline to 8.3 GtCO2 by 2030 – despite expected industrial production growth. 
  
Environmental benefits stem from the capacity to reduce harmful emissions. Depending on the location, according to 
the IEA-SHC a 1.4 MWth (2000 m²) system could generate the equivalent of 1.1 MWhth /year, a saving of around 175 
Mt of CO2.  The substitution of the fossil fuel used as heat sources by the ASTEP system will contribute, in the 
future, to the reduction of the CO2 emissions. 
KPI: 6: Avoiding 5.7 t of CO2 yearly in each case study. 
 
A reduced dependency on fossil fuels and a reduction of emission of air pollutants are expected. The heat demand 
plays a major role for the industrial energy demand as it accounts for 75 % of its useful energy consumption. This heat 
is needed in several industry sectors at low and medium temperatures, which is the major requirement for the utilization 
of solar thermal energy. 
 
In European countries 75 TWh per year represents the potential of solar heat for industrial processes.  This 
would represent approximately a 4% of the industrial heat demand. On a global scale the potential of solar thermal 
heat for industrial processes can be estimated on 1,556 TWh of process heat for industry in 2050. 
 
A large share of the heat demand belo w 100°C can be met with Solar Heat for Industrial Processes ( SHIP) systems 
using improved and adopted current technologies, if suitable integration of the solar heating system can be identified. 
With ASTEP´s R&I and technological development, more and more  medium temperature applications, up to 
400°C, will also become feasible. According to a study (Ecoheatcool 2006), around 57% of the total industrial heat 
demand is required at temperatures below 400°C and 30% at temperatures below 100°C. ASTEP carries the  
technology beyond the state-of-art by integrating SunDial and latent thermal energy storage.  
KPI 7: saving 2 t of natural gas, yearly, in each case study. 
KPI 8; avoiding 5 kg of NOx (compared to uncontrolled burners), yearly, in each case study 
 
Create significant visibility to the potential of applying solar thermal energy in industrial processes . 
ASTEP aims to contribute to extend the use of thermal solar energy to industrial processes implementing 
innovative, reliable and advanced technologies to assu re a reliable solution needed for continuous operating 
process. ASTEP also aims to support additional projects beyond the demonstrators providing insights for the project’s 
development and to further create opportunities for the replication of the demonstrators. 
 
ASTEP aims at successfully demonstrate the employ of solar energy for industrial purposes resulting in improved 
solutions compared to the currently available.  This is system will be implanted in two different climate regions : 
Corinth in Greece, which is located at a latitude where the use of solar energy is more widespread, and also it 
is planned to be installed at Iasi in Romania, located at latitudes where its use is less common. This aspect will 
also contribute to increment the visibility in EU countries where such systems currently have very limited or 
no application. 
 
The successful completion of ASTEP will allow providing guidelines for planning, installation and operation of 
thermal solar systems in industrial companies, quantifying the performance of the innovative technology 
implemented considering several technical and economic parameters. This multi-criteria evaluation enables an 
overall view on the innovative technology proposed regarding different sustainability criteria.  
KPI 9: dissemination deliverables of project results targeted to different stakeholders and countries.  
 
Contribute to the development of relevant Best Available Techniques Reference Document (BREF) under the 
Industrial Emissions Directive.  A life cycle assessment (LCA) will be performed in line with the rules of the 
International Reference Life Cycle Data System (ILCD) and in line with international standards. These multi -criteria 
assessments on several sustainability criteria allow to judge over alternative solutions developed in the framework of 
this project and provide support to identify the  best solution for further technical development towards demonstration 
in follow -up projects within industry. The development of more resource efficient and environmentally friendly 
technology improves the quality of life in a sustainable way. The LCA will  verify the substantially lowering of the 
environmental, health & safety impact. 
 
KPI 10: WP8 incorporates five (5) relevant deliverables (D8.1-8.5) regarding sustainable assessment.  
In this sense, the results of these analysis will contribute to the development of BREF guides to exchange information 
among the industrial sectors and  the European Integrated Pollution Prevention and Control Bureau (IPCC/EIPPCB).

2.1.2 Empowering the European solar thermal related and energy storage industry (including SMEs) 
Although Europe is making good progress towards its 2020 targets for solar electricity, the heating and cooling sector 
risks missing the indicative target for 2020 by 19.5%. If this trend is to be reversed, t hen several challenges will have 
to be overcome, including extending the market into new segments, such as industrial. Solar Heat for Industrial 
Processes (SHIP) is a promising market for solar thermal applications. According to the European Solar Thermal 
Industrial Federation, currently over 190 operating solar thermal systems for process heat are reported worldwide, with 
a total capacity of over 111 MWth (159 000 m²). In the Solar Heating and Cooling Technology Roadmap is mentioned 
that most of these syst ems are pilot plants with a relatively small size. However, there is great potential for market 
developments based on innovations, as 28% of the overall energy demand in the EU28 countries originates in the 
industrial sector and most of this heat demand is  in the temperature range below 250°C. The estimated thermal solar 
sector turnover in Europe is 1.9€ billion and the number of jobs of the sector is around 23700. 
 
Regarding energy costs, and potential savings, there are three main aspects to consider that  have a bigger impact on 
the comparable costs of the energy produced by a solar thermal system. These are the initial cost of the system, the 
lifetime of the system and the system performance. These factors depend on the location (affecting climate, insulation, 
taxes, cost of living, etc.) and quality of the system (affecting performance, lifetime and cost). This can vary 
significantly from country to country. Therefore, average investment costs for solar thermal systems can vary greatly 
from country to country and between different systems. According to the IEA ( Fig. 2.1), for large systems in Europe, 
the investment costs can go from 350 to 1040 USD/kWth (315 to 936 EUR/kWth). In terms of energy costs, it can 
range from 20 to 70 USD/ MWhth (18 to 63 EUR/MWh th) in Southern United States and between 40 and 150 
USD/MWhth  (36 and 135 EUR/MWhth) in Europe. 
 
 
Figure 2.1: Costs of solar heating and cooling 
 
ASTEP system will have an important impact in cost reduction allowing its worldwide applicability and making 
it feasible for different climatic conditions and for a wide range of process temperatures up to 400 °C. 
 
A specific task will be to identify the main priorities for future research, as well as a series of applications (pilot and 
demonstration projects) that can build upon ASTEP results. This will lead to a sustainable, long-term framework, thus 
enabling a more competitive market for solar energy technologies. In line with the objectives of the work programme 
ASTEP will contribute t o meeting the overarching objective of the EU research policy to develop an open and 
competitive European Research Area (ERA), which is a core element in the research and innovation agenda of the 
Commission, as defined in the Innovation Union flagship. 
The European science community and economy will benefit greatly from ASTEP results. The outcome will help 
address the needs of EU science policy in solar energy use and meet the key challenge faced by concentrated solar 
power. The impact of the project activity will be immediate in both academia and industry by providing the best solution 
for the use of concentrated solar energy. This should help scientists and engineers in designing concentrated solar 
power plants in a way to reduce their cost and improve performance. European consumers will also benefit from clean 
energy and environment. 
 
ASTEP project through the integration of innovative technology developments represents a competitive 
advantage for empowering the European solar thermal related industry (including SMEs) with respect to Asian 
competitors.  
KPI 1,2: two representative industrial demo-site from different climate conditions and different industrial sectors will 
be operating throughout the project, providing the most valuable know-how on market extension to industrial sector. 
 
2.1.3 Environmental, social and other impacts 
EU Policies. In October 2014, EU Member States agreed on ambitious EU -wide climate and energy targets for 2030: 
a 40% cut in greenhouse gas emissions compared to 1990 levels; at least a 27% share of renewable energy consumption; 
This proposal version was submitted by Antonio ROVIRA on 27/08/2019 13:57:53 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
  LC-RES-07-2019 
Page 19 
and at least 27% energy savings compared with the business -as-usual scenario. The Paris Agreement, which was 
approved at the Conferences of the Parties (COP21) in December 2015 and became legally binding in November 2016 
following its ratification, requires the EU to further strengthen its 2030 energy and climate framework through 
legislative action. It also steers the entire global community on a path to decarbonisation, which will increase the global 
need for low-carbon energy generation and therefore also for low-carbon balancing and flexibility means.   
To implement the goals of the Energy Union and to advance the energy transition, the EU issued the “Clean Energy 
for all Europeans” package in November  2016. This includes several key pieces of legislation: the important 
amendments to the Third Energy Package known as Energy Market Design; the Accelerating Clean Energy Innovation 
communication; the new Renewable Energy Directive; the Directive on the EU Emissions Trading System; and the 
Directive on Energy Efficiency. ASTEP will contribute to lower environmental impact, contributing to facing 
energy challenges. (KPIs 6-7) 
 
Environmental attitude and social awareness. ASTEP, also contributes to an overall improvement of environmental 
attitude by raising awareness of the great potential of introducing an integrated, reliable and economical feasible 
solution to cover by means of solar thermal energy the highest possible share of the heating and/or cooling demand of 
representative industrial processes. Within this visibility potential, EU countries where the penetration of this 
technologies is very limited will be addressed. 
KPIs 9-10: dissemination deliverables of project results targeted to different stakeholders and countries. 
 
Political, social and economic benefits.  They are associated with the potential savings in energy costs and the 
possibility of improving energy security by reducing energy imports, while creating local j obs related to the 
manufacturing, commercialization, installation and maintenance of solar thermal systems. 
ASTEP will also contribute to employment from jobs created in system design, equipment manufacturing, 
operation and maintenance. Advantageously, man y of these jobs cannot be delocalized such as operation and 
maintenance.

2.2 Measures to maximize impact 
2.2.1 Dissemination and exploitation of results 
A comprehensive communication and dissemination package is key to increase the project's impacts. The consortium 
will follow a comprehensive strategy, using different means of dissemination including the organization of several 
workshops, webinars, a comprehensive set of project deliverables, oral and poster presentations at different events, the 
project’s website and e-newstletters etc. In order to maximize the impact of ASTEP, target oriented activities will be 
This proposal version was submitted by Antonio ROVIRA on 27/08/2019 13:57:53 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
  LC-RES-07-2019 
Page 20 
conducted to disseminate the research and innovation results to the widest possible audience (general public, research 
community, targeted market, industry and governmental stakeholders…).   
Dissemination is a critical task in any research project. Any effort and resources invested will be useless, unless the 
benefits of the project can reach the European society who funded this initiative. Besides, an effective dissemination 
plan contributes to weakening the barriers that prevent the achievement of the targeted impact. In order to plan an 
effective strategy for dissemination, EU’s five-stage model indications will be followed: 
 
Stage 1: To make a clear selection of objectives of dissemination.  
 
The main dissemination objectives and goals are as follows:  
• To raise awareness on the target audiences, and particularly the relevant stakeholders, policy makers, potentia l 
users and market segments, about the objectives of the project, its results, its benefits, use and applicability. 
• To get the necessary feedback to investigate the needs of the market and create new needs.  
• To seek the support of the general public, end-users, organizations, authorities, lobbyists and policy makers.  
• To achieve collaborations with other stakeholders in the technical, commercial and corporative fields with the aim 
to share resources, create synergies and exchange information and knowledge.  
• To identify end-user organizations willing to validate the final project outcomes.  
• To support the exploitation by finding potential customers.  
• To promote and create market opportunities that might result in sales.  
• To promote agreements with commercial partners.  
• To promote agreements with investors.  
• To prepare the ground for product launching. 
These objectives will be reached through a strategy based in the identification and mobilization of all potential 
stakeholders, from scientific community to policy makers via the end-users of the technological solution developed in 
this project. 
 
Stage 2: To identify the results of dissemination and its suitability for the selected audience.  
The message to be disseminated will be strongly dependent on the moment of the project lifetime when the 
dissemination action occurs. Timing is critical for effective dissemination to coordinate dissemination with the progress 
of the project, IPR protection and exploitation. Planning considers not only timing but an adequate correlation between 
the dissemination channel, the message and the targeted audience. In this sense, three dissemination levels have been 
defined:  
• Level 1- Raise awareness within stakeholders about the project and its objectives. Actions on this level will be 
carried out at the beginning of the project.  
• Level 2 - Promote the results of the project. These actions will take place from the second half of the Year 2 to the 
end of the project.  
• Level 3 - Prepare the ground for exploitation of the project results. These dissemination actions will serve to support 
activities in Task 9.4 “Exploitation Strategy” and will occur during all the lifetime of the project.  
Appropriate targeted audience and channels will be selected  in function of these objectives and levels in order to 
maximize the impact of the actions. 
 
Stage 3: To communicate stakeholders their responsibilities on dissemination.  
Each beneficiary shall ensure that the foreground of which it has ownership is disseminated as swiftly as possible. 
Dissemination activities shall be compatible with the protection of intellectual property rights, confidentiality 
obligations and the legitimate interests of the owner(s) of the foreground. Conditions and procedure for dissemination 
will be reflected in the Grant Agreement signed by all the consortium members prior to the beginning of the project. 
To be developed in Task 9.1 “Communication Strategy”. 
 
Stage 4: To Identify appropriate channels for dissemination.  
Due to the high innovation degree of the ASTEP project the protection of IPR is of paramount importance to this 
project. As such, the consortium members recognize that any dissemination  will only be carried out after the IPR 
generated by the project has been protected by means of patents or other appropriate vehicles. However, it is also 
recognized that successful dissemination is crucial to ensure successful exploitation of the project results, requiring 
therefore a solid planning of all the activities carried by each partner. These plans will be addressed throughout the 
entire project duration. The dissemination activities planned in ASTEP project aim at bringing and pushing the results 
directly to the ASTEP targeted stakeholders, end users and wider scientific community. A detailed dissemination plan 
will be drawn at the pertinent working tasks. Some activities for an effective dissemination are already planned to 
This proposal version was submitted by Antonio ROVIRA on 27/08/2019 13:57:53 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
  LC-RES-07-2019 
Page 21 
include publications, press releases and the active participation in conferences, workshops, platforms and fairs (Table 
2.1). To be developed in Task 9.2 “Dissemination Strategy”. 
 
Stage 5: To monitor and evaluate the effects of the activity.  
Effective dissemination requires careful management of the expectations of the audience and this, in turn demands 
permanent monitoring of project progress and of the impact of the dissemination actions in order to validate its success 
or, in case targets are not achieved, to implement corrective actions. A dissemination and communication plan will be 
delivered in M6 and updated in M24 and M48. 
All of the dissemination and communication activities will be directed for the following recipients: academic society 
majoring in the field of solar thermal energy, representatives of industry stakeholders, regional and national authorities 
and interested citizens of the regions involved as well as students on every step of education. The outcome of the 
proposed actions is believed to create a significant footprint in the awareness of the above listed societies about how 
the solar thermal energy storage will affect the overall effectiveness and quality of research and industrial processes. 
Among different outreach events and dissemination activities the following can be surely distinguished: publications, 
organization of cascade workshops or webinars for the  Academia and Industry , opening of expert’s  lectures and 
workshops for students and citizen s, as well as extension of the teaching program  in some of the consortium’s 
institutions. Publications are planned to be published only in peer-reviewed journals with high Impact Factor. Teaching 
program of some of the incorporated partners will be broadened in the project’s area on the base of the gained advanced 
and enriching knowledge. In order to maximize the range of the influence effecting from the project’s rea lization the 
variety of training will be offered by the p roject participant organizations. The main goal of such cascade trainings is 
to ensure that the project’s realization will support not only the participating scientists, but also their departments and 
universities and the national or international national industry representatives. For that purpose, the full offer of free of 
charge trainings, webinars, lectures and workshops connected with the solar thermal energy will be claimed on the 
project’s website. If interested the companies, even if not involved in ASTEP, could get in touch with the project 
members and order such services. In case of the appearance of any industry originated problems also the consulting 
services may be offered to companies. This approach aims at maximizing of the project’s visibility and influence on 
relevant communities. It should also benefit with the number of grant proposals submitted in the future as potential 
collaborators might be gained. Communication activities will be moreover focused on: publishing of the e-newsletters 
of the project (2 issues per year) and e stablishing and keeping up -to-date the informational website of the project. In 
order to reach the widest possible audience also the social media will be covered by e.g. Twitter or Facebook account 
related to the project which will be established and managed. Every activity undertaken throughout the project will be 
firmed by the name of the project and project funding details.  
All the partners will contribute to create visibility regarding the potential of employing solar thermal energy in 
industrial processes. In order to achieve this target, dissemination actions will be carried out at different levels: 
• Scientific level (attending scientific conferences and publishing articles on indexed journals). An open access 
dissemination strategy will be followed according to the EU rules. Gold open access will be chosen as main open 
access route. 
• General public dissemination: information from the partners will be regularly gathered in order to create materials 
easy to understand and attractive for society. These will be released through the website, by press releases, sharing 
videos, or in local and national events in which the partners can participate/organize. 
• Industry oriented dissemination activities will be carried out by attending the most relevant trade fairs in the sector 
and developing suitable materials/presentations and organizing several cascade trainings or workshops. Targeted 
meetings involving the managing teams of national and multinational companies relevant in the field, 
manufacturers and possible fi nal users are foreseen  in order to present the benefits of ASTEP to key users. 
Furthermore, a targeted Workshop will be organized in one of the demo sites to present the research and innovations 
actions generated through ASTEP and show the potential of applying solar thermal energy in industrial processes. 
The event will target all possible beneficiaries of the project - from private companies, to local authorities, to public 
institutions, investors and NGOs. This workshop will be also focused on widening t he use of applying ASTEP 
technologies in those EU countries where such systems currently have very limited or no application. Specific 
materials will be developed to distribute to public authorities form different EU countries. Also, the inclusion 
within the workshops of policy and decision makers and relevant stakeholders is targeted to contribute to the future 
development of relevant EU policies such as BREFs under the Industrial Emissions Directive. 
• A final workshop will be held in order to ensure the gr eatest level of impact, a milestone event ( MS16) will be 
organized to present the project final results and to ensure rising awareness of the viability of applying the solar 
thermal solutions in industry demonstrated by ASTEP. This event will be also targe ted to a wide public (local 
stakeholders, industry, local authorities and interested citizens)

Exploitation plan  
The overall objective of the exploitation activities is to guide the project and support partners exploitation aspirations 
with a viable exploitation strategy on  a global scale. An initial assessment of the project’s exploitation options has 
been made and includes an Outline of project’s exploitable results and individual business plans as can be seen in the 
next section.  Exploitation issues will be brought to the attention of all partners from very early in the project, to ensure 
that findings regarding market and commercialization that come up when studying exploitation options can still be fed 
into the R&D part of the project. The consortium already appoints exploitation manager, Mrs. Mónica Osorio (EXE), 
who will be responsible for coordinating these actions. The Exploitation Plan will be prepared considering the real 
benefits of the project, with the aim to achieve target TRLs.  The consortium partners will work with the exploitation 
manager in the description of project results and the commercial applic ations and ownership of the results will be 
adequately identified, listed and recorded. Exploitation plan will be essentially supported with an initial market analysis 
and development of the future business model. The analysis methodology will be based on the categorization of every 
result and value chain analysis, depending whether they can be commercialized or not, and if they must be protected 
according to the Intellectual Property Rights (IPR) management policy.  
 
As part of the exploitation activities several different reports and studies will be completed. These include exploitation 
plan and IPR roadmap. The first version of the exploitation plan will be prepared during the 1st year of the project and 
it will be updated in every 6 months  and will be delivered as part of the M24 and M48 . The project aims at obtaining 
of TRL 5, which stands the technology validated in relevant environments (2 case studies at end-user’s sites). 
Nevertheless, the final version of the Exploitation Plan will be ready and put into action setting the guideline for future 
exploitation beyond the scope of the project as  a preliminary market take -up strategy and long term stability of the 
proposed solutions will be considered and elaborated by the means of ASTEP. Such approach will allow outcomes of 
the project to be fully exploited and prepared for the commercialization process. The routes to exploitation will be done 
This proposal version was submitted by Antonio ROVIRA on 27/08/2019 13:57:53 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
  LC-RES-07-2019

by several approaches including, further internal research, collaborative research and licensing. Specific exploitation
